1. **General Structure**:

In Arabic grammar, the particle "Ø£Ù†" (an) introduces a subordinate clause in the subjunctive mood. This particle is commonly used after verbs and expressions that imply necessity, possibility, or intention.

Hereâ€™s how it works:

1. **Use with Verbs**: After certain verbs that express wishing, wanting, advising, etc., "Ø£Ù†" is followed by a verb in the subjunctive mood (jussive form). For example:

     ("It was agreed that the student should go to the library.")

2. **Alternative with MaSdar**: Instead of using a subordinate clause introduced by "Ø£Ù†," you can use the verbal noun (maSdar) of the verb. For example:
   - Ø§ØªÙÙ‚ Ø¹Ù„Ù‰ Ø°Ù‡Ø§Ø¨ Ø§Ù„Ø·Ø§Ù„Ø¨ Ø¥Ù„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù…ÙƒØªØ¨Ø©.

The Arabic language has a rich system of verb conjugation that includes both active and passive voices. In your text, we see examples of how verbs are transformed into their negative forms in the past tense for various pronouns.

### Key Points from Your Text:

1. **Verb Forms**: The text discusses verbs in different forms, specifically focusing on:
   - **Active Voice**: This is the standard form where the subject performs the action.
   - **Passive Voice**: Here, the subject receives the action. It's formed by altering the internal vowel patterns of the verb.

2. **Negative Conjugation**: The text provides examples of how verbs are conjugated into their negative forms in the past tense:
   - For different pronouns such as "we" (Ù†ÙØ­Ù’ Ù†Ù), "you (plural, masculine)" (Ø£ÙÙ†Ù’ØªÙÙ…Ù’), "you (plural, feminine)" (Ø£ÙÙ†Ù’ØªÙÙ†ÙÙ‘), and "they (masculine)" (Ù‡ÙÙ…Ù’).
   - The negative particle used is Ù’Ù„ÙÙ…Ù’, which precedes the verb.

3. **Types of Verbs**:
   - **Intransitive Verbs**: These do not require a direct object (e.g., to laugh, to return).
   - **Transitive Verbs**: These require a direct object (e.g., to drink, to eat).

4. **Passive Voice Formation**:
   - Derived from transitive verbs.
   - Formed by internal vowel changes in the verb structure.

5. **Usage of Passive Voice**:
   - Used when the agent (performer) is unknown or unimportant.
   - Can apply to certain intransitive verbs that require prepositions.

### Examples:

- **Active Voice**: 
  - "We laughed" becomes "Ù†ÙØ­Ù’ Ù†Ù Ø£ÙØ¶Ù’Ø­Ù ÙƒÙÙ†Ø§" (we did not laugh).
  
- **Passive Voice**:
  - "It was built" could be formed from the active "it built."

The text provides a foundational look at how verbs are manipulated to express different voices and tenses, particularly focusing on the negative past tense. This knowledge helps in constructing meaningful sentences that accurately convey the intended message.

In Arabic grammar, conditional sentences are an essential component that convey hypothetical scenarios and their potential outcomes. These sentences consist of two main parts: a subordinate clause (Ø¬Ù…Ù„Ø© Ø§Ù„Ø´Ø±Ø·) beginning with a conditional particle, and a main clause indicating the consequence or result (Ø¬ÙˆØ§Ø¨ Ø§Ù„Ø´Ø±Ø·). Conditional particles in Arabic can be categorized based on whether they are followed by specific verb forms.

### Types of Conditional Particles:

1. **Particles Followed by Varying Verb Tenses:**
   - **Ø¥Ø°Ø§ (idha)**: This particle means "if" or "when," indicating a condition that is possible and likely to occur. It can be followed by verbs in the past tense, negated jussive form, future tense, imperative mood, etc.
     - Example: 
       - Ø¥Ø°Ø§ Ø³Ø§ÙØ±Øª Ø¥Ù„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù‚Ø§Ù‡Ø±Ø©ØŒ Ø±Ø¨Ù…Ø§ Ø²Ø±Øª Ø§Ù„Ù…ØµØ± Ø§Ù„ÙƒØ¨Ø±Ù‰. (If I travel to Cairo, I might visit the Great Pyramid.)
   - **Ù„Ùˆ (law)**: This particle indicates a hypothetical condition contrary to fact and can be used with verbs in the past tense or negated jussive form.
     - Example:
       - Ù„Ùˆ ÙƒØ§Ù† Ù„Ø¯ÙŠÙ‘ Ø§Ù„Ù…Ø§Ù„ØŒ Ø³Ø§Ø¹Ø¯ØªÙ‡. (If I had money, I would help him.)

2. **Particles Followed by Jussive Verbs Only:**
   - These include particles such as Ù…ÙÙ†Ù’ (man), Ù…ÙØ§ (maa), and others that require the use of jussive verbs.
     - Example:
       - Ù…ÙÙ†Ù’ ÙŠØ£ØªÙŠØŒ ÙŠÙØ±Ø­Ø¨ Ø¨Ù‡. (Whoever comes will be welcomed.)

3. **Particles Allowing Different Verb Forms Besides Jussive:**
   - **Ø¥Ù†Ù‘ (inna)**: This particle can be followed by jussive or indicative verbs.
     - Example:
       - Ø¥ÙÙ†Ù’ ÙŠÙƒÙ† Ø°Ù„Ùƒ ØµØ­ÙŠØ­Ø§Ù‹ØŒ Ø³Ø£Ø¹Ù…Ù„ Ø¹Ù„Ù‰ ØªØµØ­ÙŠØ­Ù‡. (If that is true, I will work on correcting it.)

### Usage of Conditional Particles:

- **Ø¥Ø°Ø§ (idha)**: As seen in the example "Ø¥Ø°Ø§ Ø³Ø§ÙØ±Øª Ø¥Ù„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù‚Ø§Ù‡Ø±Ø©ØŒ Ø±Ø¨Ù…Ø§ Ø²Ø±Øª Ø§Ù„Ù…ØµØ± Ø§Ù„ÙƒØ¨Ø±Ù‰," it introduces a realistic possibility or condition and can be paired with various verb forms to express future, past, or hypothetical actions.
  
- **Ù„Ùˆ (law)**: This is used for conditions that are contrary to reality or have not happened. It typically implies regret or speculation about an alternative outcome.

### Conclusion:

Understanding the use of conditional particles in Arabic requires recognizing which verbs they govern and their implications on the sentence's meaning. The particle "Ø¥Ø°Ø§" is commonly used when there's a likelihood of the condition being fulfilled, while "Ù„Ùˆ" deals with hypotheticals that are not true or have not occurred. ### MaSdars: Understanding Verbal Nouns in Arabic

MaSdars, or verbal nouns, are a crucial component of the Arabic language, functioning similarly to nouns but derived from verbs. They encapsulate the essence of actions and states expressed by verbs, allowing for nuanced expression in both written and spoken forms.

#### Key Characteristics of MaSdars:

1. **Derivation**: MaSdars originate from verbs and can be formed from regular tri-consonantal verbs as well as more complex ones.
2. **Flexibility**: Like nouns, maSdars can take definite articles, suffix pronouns, and change in number (singular, dual, plural).
3. **Functions**:
   - Can act like a noun in syntax.
   - Retain the preposition of the original verb or accept direct objects if derived from transitive verbs.

- **Cardinal Numbers 3-10**: These numbers have different masculine and feminine forms. The feminine form is used when the noun they modify is feminine.
